    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="review_2084105403" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2084105403" />
    <a title="Jacob Overmark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41670556-jacob-overmark"><img alt="Jacob Overmark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595533530p2/41670556.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2084105403?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob Overmark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob Overmark" href="/user/show/41670556-jacob-overmark">Jacob Overmark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753513828" href="/book/show/3038407-notes-of-a-dirty-old-man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41670556-jacob-overmark?shelf=contemporary-international">contemporary-international</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41670556-jacob-overmark?shelf=read-owned">read-owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41670556-jacob-overmark?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2084105403" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11563472182642785587">Dear Charles Bukowski (may you rot in peace) <br/><br/>You had a way with words, and your powers of observation were not without an edge. <br/>I quite liked your political statements, they showed that you after all used your intellect, what-ever-much was left of it in your intoxicated brain.<br/><br/>Your diary reminds me a bit of Celine, and maybe that was your intention, you were a man who had read a great deal.<br/><br/>I realise that you in your life have felt betrayed and not as valued a writer as you thought you deserved. <br/> </span>
  <span id="freeText11563472182642785587" style="display:none">Dear Charles Bukowski (may you rot in peace) <br /><br />You had a way with words, and your powers of observation were not without an edge. <br />I quite liked your political statements, they showed that you after all used your intellect, what-ever-much was left of it in your intoxicated brain.<br /><br />Your diary reminds me a bit of Celine, and maybe that was your intention, you were a man who had read a great deal.<br /><br />I realise that you in your life have felt betrayed and not as valued a writer as you thought you deserved. <br /> <br />Regrettably this piece of apeshit does not add much to the value. History is filled with tales of men falling apart, finding only short-lasting pleasures in sex and drugs, and describing it in detail, as if anyone cared about yet another low-life writer. <br /><br />Grammar-wise, there is something called “capital letter after punctuation” which you choose to neglect totally throughout your diary. This was very annoying, and frankly, you are not in position to set up your own grammar rules. <br /><br />However, you did provoke a smile here and there, mostly when you were sober, and I will grant you 3 small stars for you many wordplays. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11563472182642785587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer25176715" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8533970189492525125">Rating books with stars, like bars or restaurants or anything else is pretty silly.<br/><br/>This was a good book to read at night while drinking pints at pubs in Seattle. A collection of "articles" from a small(?) paper in Los Angeles (?), there's no apparent chronology or order of any kind to them. If there is, it's deep an intellectual.<br/><br/>And after all, an intellectual takes something simple and makes it complex, while an artist takes something complex and makes it simple. (Indirect book quote)<br/><br/>I'm reminde</span>
  <span id="freeText8533970189492525125" style="display:none">Rating books with stars, like bars or restaurants or anything else is pretty silly.<br /><br />This was a good book to read at night while drinking pints at pubs in Seattle. A collection of "articles" from a small(?) paper in Los Angeles (?), there's no apparent chronology or order of any kind to them. If there is, it's deep an intellectual.<br /><br />And after all, an intellectual takes something simple and makes it complex, while an artist takes something complex and makes it simple. (Indirect book quote)<br /><br />I'm reminded of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8908.World_War_Z_An_Oral_History_of_the_Zombie_War" title="World War Z An Oral History of the Zombie War by Max Brooks" rel="nofollow noopener">World War Z</a>; each story is captivating, but you tire a little of the style. However just as you're tiring of the style, you get caught up in the next story.<br /><br />I bought my dad a copy of this book... he told me it reminded him of Kerouac. That set me back a little as I never knew my father before his grumpiness and angst, his tiredness. Like a quote from Field of Dreams.<br /><br />It's also a kind of life I appreciate, which, perhaps is easy to take the wrong way. Because life is for the living. </span>

<div id="review_1620591700" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1620591700" />
    <a title="Chris_P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44309339-chris-p"><img alt="Chris_P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558554241p2/44309339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1620591700?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris_P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris_P" href="/user/show/44309339-chris-p">Chris_P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44309339-chris-p?shelf=classics-and-modern-classics">classics-and-modern-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1620591700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11544680313944544073">Picture an alien. Let's say, for convenience sake, he has a completely human appearance. He crashed on earth a few hours ago and now wanders around in a city whose name is irrelevant, while trying to get used to the possibility of having to spend the rest of his life among humans. See him as he walks through a park absorbing images and smells, pausing every once in a while to take a closer look at whatever catches his attention. Starting to feel tired, he heads toward a bench. Just before he sit</span>
  <span id="freeText11544680313944544073" style="display:none">Picture an alien. Let's say, for convenience sake, he has a completely human appearance. He crashed on earth a few hours ago and now wanders around in a city whose name is irrelevant, while trying to get used to the possibility of having to spend the rest of his life among humans. See him as he walks through a park absorbing images and smells, pausing every once in a while to take a closer look at whatever catches his attention. Starting to feel tired, he heads toward a bench. Just before he sits, he sees an object on the bench which recognizes as a book (let's again suppose that there are books on his planet and that he knows how to read). He picks it up and reads the title. "Notes of a Dirty Old Man" by Charles Bukowski. Having all the time in the world at his disposal, he starts reading. Half an hour passes and judging by the volume of the pages read and those left, we can assume he's halfway through. Now, if you look close, you'll see a certain expression on his face. Something which you recognize. Something that looks like disgust mixed with something else... Is it fear? Could it be fear? It's not a horror novel but yes, the more you look the more certain you become. It is fear. Now you see him lowering the book and looking straight ahead without really seeing much. It's the same look a convict might have. He closes the book and puts it back on the bench, at the exact same spot where he found it. Without taking his eyes from that nonexistent spot straight ahead, he reaches into the inside pocket of his coat and takes out a small object. It takes you a few seconds to realize it looks suspiciously similar to a handgun. It's probably the intergalactic equivalent of a beretta or something like that. Without even blinking he points the gun to his own head. A split second before he pulls the trigger, you see a tear just starting to trickle down his cheek. The one thought that pops in your mind as the image fades out is that his blood is red. Why is his blood red?<br /><br /><br />So, I guess I chose a somewhat extravagant way to say that if an alien's first touch with humanity was through this book, they'd totally kill themselves without thinking. Bukowski's stories paint a sick picture of everyday life. It seems like he strips reality of all that's good or pure, leaving only the rotten parts and throws it in your face. I think I would have liked it more when I was younger. However, there's something sickly poetic and poetically sick in this book which I found brilliant.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11544680313944544073" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_417052943" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/417052943" />
    <a title="Karky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3048857-karky"><img alt="Karky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1265638227p2/3048857.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/417052943?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karky" href="/user/show/3048857-karky">Karky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer417052943" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2904919788963377189">I had a Chuck Bukowski kick back in 2004. I moved out of my father's house the year prior, inadvertently isolated myself from most of my friends, got a soul-draining job in retail, and fallen into a bit of depression (which hardly anyone noticed). Though I hardly remember what I read, it spoke to me because I was in a toxic environment and Bukowski wrote with an honest intensity that was lacking in my life at the time.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_510576355" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/510576355" />
    <a title="Elise" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4922926-elise"><img alt="Elise" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491356414p2/4922926.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/510576355?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elise" href="/user/show/4922926-elise">Elise</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4922926-elise?shelf=addiction-literature">addiction-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer510576355" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6111611331290753242">This one is not for the faint of heart, the delicate, nor the easily offended, but if you are bold, daring, and curious, it is somewhat entertaining. The rants, stories, and prose poems collected here are uneven in quality, but enough of them held my interest so that I was able to finish reading "Notes of a Dirty Old Man." Bukowski is so unflinchingly honest about everything, so don't read this if you can't handle the truth. He also exposes things some might wish they had never seen--prostitutio</span>
  <span id="freeText6111611331290753242" style="display:none">This one is not for the faint of heart, the delicate, nor the easily offended, but if you are bold, daring, and curious, it is somewhat entertaining. The rants, stories, and prose poems collected here are uneven in quality, but enough of them held my interest so that I was able to finish reading "Notes of a Dirty Old Man." Bukowski is so unflinchingly honest about everything, so don't read this if you can't handle the truth. He also exposes things some might wish they had never seen--prostitution, murder, abuse, rape, suicide, suicide by alcoholism, even necrophilia. At times, I found the collection sophomoric, as though he were daring me to read on, and read on I did. While some of the stories successfully disturbed, there were also some priceless nuggets of wisdom in these pages about the futility of revolution and in this Socratic exchange, about how this world makes us all mad: <br /><br />"you say you often feel this madness, what do you do when it comes upon you?"<br /><br />"I write poetry."<br /><br />"is poetry madness?"<br /><br />"non-poetry is madness."<br /><br />"what is madness?"<br /><br />"madness is ugliness" (178).<br /><br />And ugliness is a fact of life, as "Notes of a Dirty Old Man" shows, page after page, but I still couldn't stop reading, because in the end, every story leads to the same place, and this is it: <br /><br />"man, I'd like to see a good pair of shoes on every man walking the streets and see that he gets a good piece of ass and a bellyfull of food too. Christ, the last piece of ass I've had was in 1966 and I've been jacking off ever since. and there just ain't no jackoff compared to that wonder hole" (69). <br /><br />And indeed, the conflict that drives this book is the search to get laid by a woman (and for the next drink, but he never seems to be without that because drink is so much easier to acquire). So, all this time, I saw Bukowski as a misogynist, when in fact he is entranced by the power of the feminine, the great "wonder hole," or should I say "wonder whole" because it's only when he is getting laid or writing that he is somewhat at peace, whole, just like every other angry, frustrated male writer from Herman Melville to D.H. Lawrence, to J.R.R. Tolkien. One man's elusive white whale or magic ring is another man's coveted "wonder hole." In fact, I'm feeling pretty empowered as a woman after reading this. Seriously, though, the misogyny of the other Beat writers of his time makes Bukowski look like a pussycat (no pun intended). </span>
  <a data-text-id="6111611331290753242" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_540814963" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/540814963" />
    <a title="Nikoline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2772226-nikoline"><img alt="Nikoline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595917617p2/2772226.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/540814963?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nikoline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nikoline" href="/user/show/2772226-nikoline">Nikoline</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">strong Bukowski fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nikoline by: </span>
      book club
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2772226-nikoline?shelf=1900s">1900s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2772226-nikoline?shelf=male-author">male-author</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2772226-nikoline?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2772226-nikoline?shelf=novel">novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer540814963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15255574943625727258"><i>Notes of a Dirty Old Man</i> by Charles Bukowski is somewhat a autobiography of Bukowski himself but also his alter ego, Henry. This novel is mainly dealing with gabling, drinking and whoring which Bukowski is really good at, but what I find him to be even better at is his way with words and that does not show its true colours in this book. <br/><br/>I read for the prose when it comes to this author, so I was very disappointed to find a lack so enormous that I could hardly get through the book. Still, the wri</span>
  <span id="freeText15255574943625727258" style="display:none"><i>Notes of a Dirty Old Man</i> by Charles Bukowski is somewhat a autobiography of Bukowski himself but also his alter ego, Henry. This novel is mainly dealing with gabling, drinking and whoring which Bukowski is really good at, but what I find him to be even better at is his way with words and that does not show its true colours in this book. <br /><br />I read for the prose when it comes to this author, so I was very disappointed to find a lack so enormous that I could hardly get through the book. Still, the writing style is very typical for Bukowski which gave some feeling homely. What I enjoyed the most about this book was the ending and not just because I could finally put the book down for good, but because of its optimism. Bukowski lead a very from-day-to-day life with very little content seen with modern society's eyes. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15255574943625727258" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_674028778" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/674028778" />
    <a title="Mat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7120830-mat"><img alt="Mat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585499177p2/7120830.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/674028778?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mat" href="/user/show/7120830-mat">Mat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7120830-mat?shelf=beat">beat</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer674028778" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3590310083996434029">Like <i>South of No North</i>, this book has its ups and downs, although I like <i>Notes of a Dirty Old Man</i> slightly better for several reasons. There are some really, really interesting and great short stories in this book and there are some really weird, messed-up ones which leave you saying or thinking WTF? <br/><br/>This is a collection of articles that Bukowski wrote in his column for OPEN CITY over about a 11-month period. <br/><br/>This book has reconfirmed for me the fact that Bukowski is best at this form of writing</span>
  <span id="freeText3590310083996434029" style="display:none">Like <i>South of No North</i>, this book has its ups and downs, although I like <i>Notes of a Dirty Old Man</i> slightly better for several reasons. There are some really, really interesting and great short stories in this book and there are some really weird, messed-up ones which leave you saying or thinking WTF? <br /><br />This is a collection of articles that Bukowski wrote in his column for OPEN CITY over about a 11-month period. <br /><br />This book has reconfirmed for me the fact that Bukowski is best at this form of writing - short stories. His poetry can be very hit and miss at times but his short story prose is more often good than bad and sometimes exceptionally fascinating and quirky.<br /><br />There is plenty of booze and debauchery in this collection. There were a few surprises here too, both good and bad. One good surprise was a short piece about Bukowski meeting Neal Cassady shortly before he died. It is well written, interesting and I think he does a nice summation of Cassady at the end of his life. He says that "Kerouac has written your other chapters". One disappointing surprise was Bukowski's opinion of Burroughs - "Burroughs is a very dull writer". He truly thinks Celine is the bee's knees. I have read some Celine and think he is a pretty good writer but terribly pessimistic and misanthropic - sounds right up Buk's lane huh?<br /><br />In conclusion, this book would be exceptionally good if it didn't contain those few really disturbing stories. I know some Buk fans will disagree but hell that's how I roll. Definitely worth the price of admission though.<br />A big thanks to the Temple University Japan (Tokyo) Library for lending me a copy of this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3590310083996434029" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

           <span class="uitext reviewText">no one really</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer43791873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2147146182384416979">I was expecting something witty and intelligent, what I got was violent, crude, misogynistic and highly unpleasant, in the beginning at least. After a few tens of pages it settles down into a more, well mostly, stable narrative; almost like Bukowski wanted to put off the reader from delving further into the book. Beneath the vulgarity, self-loathing and woman hating, there is a glimmer of something. Perhaps it is, as the reviews on the back cover suggest, about the futility of life. It could be </span>
  <span id="freeText2147146182384416979" style="display:none">I was expecting something witty and intelligent, what I got was violent, crude, misogynistic and highly unpleasant, in the beginning at least. After a few tens of pages it settles down into a more, well mostly, stable narrative; almost like Bukowski wanted to put off the reader from delving further into the book. Beneath the vulgarity, self-loathing and woman hating, there is a glimmer of something. Perhaps it is, as the reviews on the back cover suggest, about the futility of life. It could be just the authors’ alter-egos desire for self harm. Maybe it is a commentary on the depths to which a down-and-out (or if you prefer a poor unfortunate who has had some bad breaks) will sink in order to avoid the real world. Or perhaps it is just the rabid ranting of an old fart of a poet trying to shock. It didn’t light my world on fire.</span>

          </span>

    <a title="M.D. Curzon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45206639-m-d-curzon"><img alt="M.D. Curzon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442986821p2/45206639.jpg" /></a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1929338255" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12411179595127302771">This is Bukowski at his visceral best - a collection of his columns originally published in 'Open City' in the 1960s. By turns hilarious, disgusting, prosaic and profound, these vignettes of distilled humanity are somehow rendered all the more powerful for the squalor and the cheap sex and the shameless alcoholism. As much as these little stories are impossible to forget, however, it is Bukowski's wry observations on life that really shine through, such as 'The difference between a brave man and</span>
  <span id="freeText12411179595127302771" style="display:none">This is Bukowski at his visceral best - a collection of his columns originally published in 'Open City' in the 1960s. By turns hilarious, disgusting, prosaic and profound, these vignettes of distilled humanity are somehow rendered all the more powerful for the squalor and the cheap sex and the shameless alcoholism. As much as these little stories are impossible to forget, however, it is Bukowski's wry observations on life that really shine through, such as 'The difference between a brave man and a coward is a coward thinks twice before jumping in the cage with a lion. The brave man doesn't know what a lion is...'</span>

<div id="review_631163969" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/631163969" />
    <a title="Rupert Owen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/931489-rupert-owen"><img alt="Rupert Owen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397581683p2/931489.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/631163969?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rupert Owen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rupert Owen" href="/user/show/931489-rupert-owen">Rupert Owen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer631163969" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6226196813645473636">It's been many years since I have returned to Henry Chinaski (Charles Bukowski) and through Notes of a Dirty Old Man, I was taken on yet another sometimes befuddling and sometimes profound reading experience. Bukowski mixes his prose with personal anecdotes and downright ridiculous absurdity, like a train wreck of thought. As he was churning these out for the Open City press, I gather Bukowski would have been writing many of the stories for his own amusement, just to see how far he could stretch</span>
  <span id="freeText6226196813645473636" style="display:none">It's been many years since I have returned to Henry Chinaski (Charles Bukowski) and through Notes of a Dirty Old Man, I was taken on yet another sometimes befuddling and sometimes profound reading experience. Bukowski mixes his prose with personal anecdotes and downright ridiculous absurdity, like a train wreck of thought. As he was churning these out for the Open City press, I gather Bukowski would have been writing many of the stories for his own amusement, just to see how far he could stretch a tale into the obscenely bizarre - as there are many in this collection that defy reality, but it is pertinent as it is free flowing intoxicating imagination peppered with some predicaments like the piece about Bukowski's parents and the Frozen Man that is quite introspectively sad. As with the story of Neal Cassady, there is something profoundly swift in the way it opens up the wounds of humanity, to drain the infection. As the title suggests, these are notes, and the man penning them is dirty minded and getting on in life. You can't argue with that.<br /><br />Bukowski has morality and ethics, but they are measured within a tawdry urban world that is collapsing inside itself. For instance his shirt cardboard reflections, 'if you want to know who your friends are, get yourself a jail sentence', in other societies and circles, the test of friendship would not be so extreme, but in Bukowski's world, a jail sentence would suffice as best a test of friendship as you can get. A writer like Wordsworth would draw for us the beauty of nature, but Bukowski points out that nature may be drawn as one thing but how it goes about its business of being natural is another thing entirely. He also speaks for the thoughts and actions of humanity that is not dogmatic idealism, some people are embarrassed when they fart, but imagine if they farted and had a follow through? This is what Bukowski is about. When the mind is roughing it, not taking the usual route. <br /><br />Notes of a Dirty Old Man has all the stickiness of ill mannered sex, sordid situations, crass thoughts, and broken down poetry, but it does feel good to read it, like taking hard liquor that burns the throat, once it hits the belly it loosens you up. This book is not for those seeking Dostoyevsky or Chekhov, tales of the poor, set in earnest poverty - if misfortune is a stream, some writers would write about people trying to get out of it, or simply being carried away by its current, but Bukowski writes about splashing, bomb diving, paddling, skinny dipping and fishing in that stream of misfortune. That's what you'll discover in its pages. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6226196813645473636" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2079617428" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2079617428" />
    <a title="Alex Jones" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34534175-alex-jones"><img alt="Alex Jones" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542525796p2/34534175.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2079617428?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex Jones" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex Jones" href="/user/show/34534175-alex-jones">Alex Jones</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2079617428&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2079617428" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9291181263379290254">Though it recycles a fair amount of "Post Office," "Ham on Rye," and "Factotum," "Notes of a Dirty Old Man" feels a little fresh for Bukowski. It at least has its own personality. You can tell he was pretty smashed while writing this shit - he'll segue from an MRA-style "women-are-conniving-rats" rant to a story about fucking a 5-foot tall, 350-pound prostitute. <br/><br/>It's raw stuff, with little to no care put in for structural cohesion. At one point, Bukowski states that he is aware that his narratio</span>
  <span id="freeText9291181263379290254" style="display:none">Though it recycles a fair amount of "Post Office," "Ham on Rye," and "Factotum," "Notes of a Dirty Old Man" feels a little fresh for Bukowski. It at least has its own personality. You can tell he was pretty smashed while writing this shit - he'll segue from an MRA-style "women-are-conniving-rats" rant to a story about fucking a 5-foot tall, 350-pound prostitute. <br /><br />It's raw stuff, with little to no care put in for structural cohesion. At one point, Bukowski states that he is aware that his narration is switching between tenses, and tells the reader that, if they care, they can "shove a nipple up their scrotum." This doesn't even make anatomical sense. <br /><br />Yeah, you can see why the FBI kept a file on Charles Bukowski for this book. At one point, someone says to a Bukowski self-insert character, "It doesn't matter whether your stories are true," to which Bukowski replies "They are." This could all be bluffing, but if not, then Bukowski has raped and beaten a good few souls in this world. If he isn't bluffing, he has coasted from a childhood of abuse and hatred to an adulthood of boozing, rape, violence, and laziness, all while maintaining interiority and literary wit.<br /><br />Of course, that doesn't make any of his literal and figurative woman-bashing acceptable, but it's part of the entrance fee for reading this shit.<br /><br />There is a sequence in "Notes of a Dirty Old Man" where a painting instructor gives a young Bukowski brushes and paints (he didn't bring his own), and instructs him to paint a vase, just like his classmates. While they take hours, he is finished in five minutes. His color is sparse and basic, and the vase resembles shit more than slightly in its coloring. But his classmates are amazed and refuse to believe Bukowski has never painted before.<br /><br />The inclusion of this story may sound pretentious, and that is probably because it is, but it is a good encapsulation of the Bukowski appeal. Even if it is all an act, all of the autobiographical shit, Bukowski still has the narrative perspective of a person who refused to be groomed by his parents, teachers, or lovers. An alcoholic, violent, reflective, melancholy, predatory, imaginative, brutal narrator. And "NOADOM" reads like a tour through his boundary-less mind.<br /><br />"NOADOM" is good Bukowski, with his penchant for crushing realistic stories, his angels-and-demons-and-necrophilia stories, his putrid sex stories, and his politically nihilistic stories. It's the sort of gut-slice writing that you either devour or spit back out in disgust.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9291181263379290254" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3993338116172306469"><i>an intellectual is a man who says a simple thing in a difficult way; an artist is a man who says a difficult thing in a simple way</i><br/><br/>I've always loved that quote. Or since I first read it anyway. But I didn't know that it came from this book.<br/><br/>I really don't know if I liked this book. I just didn't get into it. And not even because it was nasty or creepy (because let's face it, despite panic attacks and crap, I made it through Brett Easton Ellis' <i>American Psycho</i> and if I can get through that, I think</span>
  <span id="freeText3993338116172306469" style="display:none"><i>an intellectual is a man who says a simple thing in a difficult way; an artist is a man who says a difficult thing in a simple way</i><br /><br />I've always loved that quote. Or since I first read it anyway. But I didn't know that it came from this book.<br /><br />I really don't know if I liked this book. I just didn't get into it. And not even because it was nasty or creepy (because let's face it, despite panic attacks and crap, I made it through Brett Easton Ellis' <i>American Psycho</i> and if I can get through that, I think I can get through any book). <br /><br />I don't know what difficult thing Bukowski was trying to say but I can't argue that it was told in a very simple way. <br /><br />This is the first of his books that I've read and I catch myself thinking that I really don't hope the rest of his book are the same. Because when I finally, after three days, managed to turn the last page of this 200 page book, I just didn't care.<br /><br />Ive heard great things about his way of writing and what he can do with words. So I hope this is just a one-off. </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/659911222?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Guillermo Galvan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Guillermo Galvan" href="/user/show/12138532-guillermo-galvan">Guillermo Galvan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753513828" href="/book/show/3038407-notes-of-a-dirty-old-man">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer659911222" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3339453933587095580">This book isn't his best stuff. His prime writing can be found elsewhere. Yet Notes of a Dirty Old Man is gem for any long time Bukowski reader because he reveals aspects of himself that are missing from his big titles. This collection of writing has a freer vibe and is more experimental. Political, post-modern, queer, themes almost untouched in his massive body of work.<br/><br/>I give this book five stars for its insightful qualities into a writer I thought could no longer surprise me.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1660687513" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1660687513" />
    <a title="Tom" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12375479-tom"><img alt="Tom" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1385173735p2/12375479.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1660687513?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom" href="/user/show/12375479-tom">Tom</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1660687513" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15850463038800785290"><br/><br/>I hadn't read any Bukowski in over a year so I thought it was about time that I carried on with my challenge which is to read everything that he's ever released.<br/><br/>Notes Of A Dirty Old Man is a compilation of columns and short stories that have been collected from Bukowski's early days when he was writing for Open City which was a free, leftist leaning magazine which had a politicalised agenda. Its main aim was to support and influence the non-conformist countercultures which were thriving througho</span>
  <span id="freeText15850463038800785290" style="display:none"><br /><br />I hadn't read any Bukowski in over a year so I thought it was about time that I carried on with my challenge which is to read everything that he's ever released.<br /><br />Notes Of A Dirty Old Man is a compilation of columns and short stories that have been collected from Bukowski's early days when he was writing for Open City which was a free, leftist leaning magazine which had a politicalised agenda. Its main aim was to support and influence the non-conformist countercultures which were thriving throughout the 60's underground of America.<br /><br />This book is definitely more political than his others. However, there's still the same old dosage of smut, filth and complete degeneracy and perverseness throughout which will satisfy any Bukowski fan. <br /><br />As with anything written by Bukowski, I wouldn't recommend it to you if you're easily offended or overtly PC. This certainly won't be an enjoyable read for you if you're either of the aforementioned.<br /><br />Various authors are name dropped throughout the novel including: Dostoyevsky, Celine, Camus, Cassady, Kerouac, Burroughs, and Ginsberg. He pays respect and champions some of them, and completely berates and slanders others, as I'm sure you'd expect.<br /><br />A few of the stories from this book were also featured in Barfly. If you haven't seen the film and you love anything Bukowski related then check it out.<br /><br />This isn't a review, it's just a long winded reminder to myself about what I've read as I have a habit of forgetting certain specifics of a book after reading it. If this serves as a catalyst towards anyone else reading it then that's cool, but I honestly couldn't really care either way.<br /><br />It's Bukowski, he's my favourite writer so I'm obviously gonna rate it 5/5. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15850463038800785290" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_416746338" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/416746338" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12020580-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345405124p2/12020580.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/416746338?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/12020580-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer416746338" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10127039908542309210">A quick background on my exposure to Bukowski:<br/><br/>A number of years ago, I watched the Bukowski-scripted movie Barfly, starring Micky Rourke as Bukowski's alter-ego, Chinaski. It's a pretty good flick, which introduces the man's proclivity for drinking and fighting, as well as his exceptional talent for writing poetry and generally being character.<br/><br/>Much later I read "Women", which is a largely autobiographical novel that once again uses the Chinaski character to see the world through Bukowski's eyes.</span>
  <span id="freeText10127039908542309210" style="display:none">A quick background on my exposure to Bukowski:<br /><br />A number of years ago, I watched the Bukowski-scripted movie Barfly, starring Micky Rourke as Bukowski's alter-ego, Chinaski. It's a pretty good flick, which introduces the man's proclivity for drinking and fighting, as well as his exceptional talent for writing poetry and generally being character.<br /><br />Much later I read "Women", which is a largely autobiographical novel that once again uses the Chinaski character to see the world through Bukowski's eyes. The man basically slummed it through large parts of his life, making money from writing when he could, but taking crap jobs to supplement his steady drinking habit. Though often poor and always rough-looking, Bukowski was never lacking for sex. He wasn't always very picky, but he usually imbues even the saddest sexual act with some small amount of beauty. <br /><br />"Notes of a Dirty Old Man" is a collection of essays/short stories/musings that Bukowski wrote for an underground Los Angeles paper in the late 1960s, just before he reached enough acclaim to be a full-time professional writer. Being that his editor barely even checked what he was writing, it's a mixed bag. Some of the entries seem incomplete or just plain bizarre or nearly incoherent. Most, though, are interesting enough to entertain or provoke thought. There are several that I found exceptionally engaging, funny, and revelatory. While some are political or philosophical rants (which can be fun or can be disturbing), some of the best are when Bukowski gets the most personal. Anyone who's seen or heard Bukowski's rare public readings would be interested to know the background that formed the oddly monotone, thoroughly-jaded man of poetic genius. <br /><br />There are also more than a few really hilarious short stories that range from sports tales to slice-of-down-and-out life that are very true to the spirit of the Beat Generation that preceded them. <br /><br />I'll definitely pick up some more Bukowski in the future, but I have to portion it out. Though he can be highly amusing, entertaining, and insightful, his was a rather haggard life that sometimes wears me down to read about. This collection gives you a little bit of almost everything that was in the man's prose repetoir. A good, quick read for anyone interested.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10127039908542309210" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_558337050" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/558337050" />
    <a title="Jack Stevenson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17975630-jack-stevenson"><img alt="Jack Stevenson" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/558337050?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jack Stevenson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jack Stevenson" href="/user/show/17975630-jack-stevenson">Jack Stevenson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer558337050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10283454374358089586">This is essentially a collection of Charles Bukowski's writings for an underground newspaper from the late 1960s. It's semi-autobiographical in that some appear to be true stories from his life, some are blatantly fictional, and some are written from the viewpoint of his alter-ego.<br/><br/>This book isn't going to be for everyone. Bukowski is an unapologetic dirty old man, as the title of the book would suggest, and positively revels in stumbling around America, drinking and fighting and having sex with </span>
  <span id="freeText10283454374358089586" style="display:none">This is essentially a collection of Charles Bukowski's writings for an underground newspaper from the late 1960s. It's semi-autobiographical in that some appear to be true stories from his life, some are blatantly fictional, and some are written from the viewpoint of his alter-ego.<br /><br />This book isn't going to be for everyone. Bukowski is an unapologetic dirty old man, as the title of the book would suggest, and positively revels in stumbling around America, drinking and fighting and having sex with many, many people. Some of the book made me feel slightly ill, and even though I'd consider myself a fan of Bukowski, the endlessly seedy nature of the book means it gets a little repetitive if you try to read lots of it in one sitting; this is best enjoyed by reading a few pages a day. <br /><br />If you can get over those hurdles though, this is a hugely entertaining read. I think I enjoyed it particularly because it offers a unique snapshot of a time and country I'm very interested in, and also because Bukowski's aforementioned lifestyle is the polar opposite to mine. Even if neither of those things apply to you though there are some beautiful, thought provoking lines hidden amongst all the dirt, and Bukowski blurs the line between fact and fiction so well that often you don't know what you're reading, which I found kind of fascinating.<br /><br />I've never been good at conclusions. Read this book if you have a strong stomach. If you don't, try to anyway because it is interesting and entertaining throughout. 4 stars!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10283454374358089586" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_238018101" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer13139912644960931469">I really don't get what makes Bukowski so profound. Notes of a Dirty Old Man is the ramblings of a drunk and dirty gambler, always looking for his next drink or screw. Bukowski's writing is called Dirty Realism. That pretty much sums this particular book up. This book is a collection of little snippets of stories, based on Bukowski's life. He has no goals or aspirations. He barely works. The only thing he seems to have going for him is a large penis, but in my opinion, he's so dirty, smelly, and</span>
  <span id="freeText13139912644960931469" style="display:none">I really don't get what makes Bukowski so profound. Notes of a Dirty Old Man is the ramblings of a drunk and dirty gambler, always looking for his next drink or screw. Bukowski's writing is called Dirty Realism. That pretty much sums this particular book up. This book is a collection of little snippets of stories, based on Bukowski's life. He has no goals or aspirations. He barely works. The only thing he seems to have going for him is a large penis, but in my opinion, he's so dirty, smelly, and disgusting that I wouldn't touch it with my enemy's vagina, just so I could ask her how he was.<br /><br />It turns out he produced a shit-load (an appropriate term) of work. Well, I am not sure I want to read much more of it. I feel like I could give any local homeless drunk in North Hollywood a typewriter and some paper and he would come up with similar stories. So maybe Bukowski is profound because he was the first to write in this kind of voice. Maybe, he is seen as an anti-hero or an outsider. That's just not enough for me.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2210089735?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer14340144482378905816">Well well, what can one say about the crooked mindset of Bukowski? Not much to be frank. It's a book full of disconnected crazy/dirty stories at first sight, but it's a solid read with his literary qualities and at times witty, at times dry sense of humour. Surely he's been through a lot in life!</span>

<div id="review_927268195" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/927268195" />
    <a title="Rob" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30667391-rob"><img alt="Rob" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596335363p2/30667391.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/927268195?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rob" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rob" href="/user/show/30667391-rob">Rob</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Rob by: </span>
      Mark L. Kidd
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30667391-rob?shelf=journalism">journalism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer927268195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5247680492135486795">Journalism? Bukowski? Technically, yes. These writings were all first published under the by-line of “Notes Of A Dirty Old Man” in a Los Angeles free weekly called “Open City” between the years of 1967 – 1969. They largely consist of the usual semi-autobiographical fare from the author in the form of short stories and a few poems but they are essentially really more of a grab bag collection of odds and ends on a variety of subjects. Naturally, due to the confines of the printed page of the newsp</span>
  <span id="freeText5247680492135486795" style="display:none">Journalism? Bukowski? Technically, yes. These writings were all first published under the by-line of “Notes Of A Dirty Old Man” in a Los Angeles free weekly called “Open City” between the years of 1967 – 1969. They largely consist of the usual semi-autobiographical fare from the author in the form of short stories and a few poems but they are essentially really more of a grab bag collection of odds and ends on a variety of subjects. Naturally, due to the confines of the printed page of the newspaper these are all fairly brief and are more sketches rather than complete stories. <br /><br />Overall, there is no real cohesion or particular story line present here. The only unity that could be said to bring these together is the distinctive narrative voice of the author and his usual themes of the darker aspects of life. Many of these articles occasionally touch on the current events of the day but largely they concern themselves with encounters he’s had with people past and present, real or imagined, while others could be even viewed as more in the vein of op-ed rants rather than any of these. <br /><br />The foreword written by Bukowski to this book pretty much tells the story best as to how he came to work for this paper, as well as what it did for his writing and reputation back before he was truly famous. I venture to add to this that at the time of these writings Bukowski had just begun to find his niche and had managed to only publish roughly eight books over the last decade none of which really brought him any widespread fame or financial security. After an initial trial period at this free weekly his every submission was accepted sight unseen and printed with absolutely no editing. With decades of rejection slips under his belt this kind of freedom was more than appreciated for the struggling writer. It also created a monster, so to speak.<br /><br />Due to this nearly unheard of and unprecedented freedom, some of his best writing can be found here. He was something of an angry and wounded caged animal that was let loose upon the streets completely unchecked and free to roam where he pleased. He also most likely felt a certain freedom due to the underground nature of this publication and thought he probably had absolutely nothing to lose by anything he wrote here. <br /><br />At it’s height “Open City” had an estimated circulation of 35,000 and gained a reputation as a place for the counterculture to voice their radical politics, opinions of rock music, as well as becoming something of a first amendment crusader. However, this last claim to fame and notoriety also garnered them the attention of the local government and it took but only two incidences of obscenity violations to put the paper out of business, which was unable to afford the fines levied against them. The death knell was actually in part due to Bukowski’s personally editing a supplement publication, which printed a story by another writer that described the sexual antics of an underage girl.<br /> <br />Not to be an apologist on his behalf but Bukowski really seemed to only gain notoriety by being becoming a pessimistic and somewhat self-defeatist writer. After trying to play the publishing game by the rules to no effect, he seemingly raged against it and can be seen to a certain degree as trying to purposefully sabotage his own potential fame through his actions with others, either in person or through his writing. It was almost as if he could only win by purposefully losing. <br /><br />Worth mentioning as a warning to anyone that has not read much of this author, this specific book holds some of his most offensive and tasteless writings that would seem to fall under his testing these boundaries of complete acceptance by this particular publication. For some, this may hold greater appeal but for others, like myself, this could have been done without in many respects. Regardless, against my better judgment I still feel it is a good read but with a just few reluctant misgivings and reservations.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5247680492135486795" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3259965809" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3259965809" />
    <a title="Bruno Guerreiro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/105698044-bruno-guerreiro"><img alt="Bruno Guerreiro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605470324p2/105698044.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3259965809?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bruno Guerreiro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bruno Guerreiro" href="/user/show/105698044-bruno-guerreiro">Bruno Guerreiro</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753513828" href="/book/show/3038407-notes-of-a-dirty-old-man">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3259965809" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer43411731209192218">Disgusting, depraved, vile, sadistic, cruel. Filled with images of filth, sex and violence, Bukowski's eulogy of "the lowlife" evokes feelings of shock and contempt, and yet it fascinates us in a weird, twisted way.<br/><br/>Loved it.<br/><br/>Edit: I'd like to add a few things to my "original" review, that I've seen found myself thinking about.<br/>I'm by no means a literary expert nor do I aspire to be, so this is entirely my opinion and my feelings towards this piece of literature, from a casual reader point of view.</span>
  <span id="freeText43411731209192218" style="display:none">Disgusting, depraved, vile, sadistic, cruel. Filled with images of filth, sex and violence, Bukowski's eulogy of "the lowlife" evokes feelings of shock and contempt, and yet it fascinates us in a weird, twisted way.<br /><br />Loved it.<br /><br />Edit: I'd like to add a few things to my "original" review, that I've seen found myself thinking about.<br />I'm by no means a literary expert nor do I aspire to be, so this is entirely my opinion and my feelings towards this piece of literature, from a casual reader point of view.<br /><br />One of the things that I've found absolutely amazing and mechancolic simultaneously was the acceptance Bukowski's characters shows toward their own condition. Very rarely do they question it and it surely doesn't seem like they regret it. His depictions are Kafka-like (I'm basing such comparion on what I think I understood from "The Metamorphosis") in the sense that his characters always seem to be put or presented in situations that are dark and/or chaotic (but not always) and no point of return. Where I feel like he differs from the aforementioned author is on the past context and past occurrences of their stories. What I mean by this is that, in Kafka's work, it seems that the past occurrences that led the narrative to reach the scene that is presented to the reader is more often than not omitted or completely absent, whereas in Bukowski's case, we know what led to the moment, but the characters just accept it, rarely questioning or regretting their actions. In a way, I feel like both authors embrace the absurd of life, in a philosophical sense, but they do so in completely different ways. Kafka seems to be melancholic towards the absurd but he accepts that sadness, Bukowski seems to be violent and revolting towards that same absurd, but he ends up accepting it too.<br />Another thing I found myself enjoying and despising at the same time was the complete misery in which Bukowski involves his characters. They are miserable, they are poor, they are hateful, they are extremist individualists to the point that you can sometimes feel an underlying nihilism of sorts, but he writes in such a paradoxical way that it becomes charming. He shows the charm and ironic beauty in the ugliness of the word and the people. He embraces the awful, the "lowlife", the "rejects of society" and presents them in an hyperbolic way. Ironically I never found myself analizing any character or social interation through blind stereotipization, even though they are intentionally (or at least I feel like they are) written that way.<br />I'd also like to point out that Bukowski's constant and fantastic use of dysphemisms and hyperbole to describe the thoughts and actions of the characters add a lot, and I do mean a whole lot, to make the reader feel tense and uncomfortable, but it never seems to shock and intentionally deter the reader to continue. If anything, I feel like it does the exact opposite, often making me think "Can this get any worse? How much pain can X or Y withstand?".<br />In closing, I found this book a very macabre experience, but a positive one nonetheless and I'm certain I'll end up reading more of his work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="43411731209192218" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10038696072122604767">Bukowksi worked well when given every writers dream: carte blanche to write whatever he felt like writing, only to have it reproduced by the LA FREE PRESS and on the streets in a week, being read by thousands. Bukowski himself writes of the magical feeling of having the freedom to write whatever he wanted and the sudden notoriety he aquired with his new exposure. This collection of stories, as opposed to the ones collected elsewhere, show him still experimenting with a variety of forms and these</span>
  <span id="freeText10038696072122604767" style="display:none">Bukowksi worked well when given every writers dream: carte blanche to write whatever he felt like writing, only to have it reproduced by the LA FREE PRESS and on the streets in a week, being read by thousands. Bukowski himself writes of the magical feeling of having the freedom to write whatever he wanted and the sudden notoriety he aquired with his new exposure. This collection of stories, as opposed to the ones collected elsewhere, show him still experimenting with a variety of forms and these stories contain a marked sense of surrealism and a even a polemical political bent, something which Bukowski usually had little stomach for; he gets in a few good ones at the expense of the false prophets of the 60's countercultural scene, a much needed antidote to the disgusting self-mythologizing that has always accompanied the free-love generation.</span>

<div id="review_1915387562" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1915387562" />
    <a title="Fiona" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49874028-fiona"><img alt="Fiona" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600023020p2/49874028.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1915387562?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fiona" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fiona" href="/user/show/49874028-fiona">Fiona</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49874028-fiona?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1915387562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer583493738964709683">This is not my favourite of Bukowski's, yet "classic Bukowski" it was indeed (I missed the old bastard). <br/><br/>I am not one to be easily offended (especially when it comes to the brutally honest, non-romantisized works of Bukowski) yet I found myself gasping in horror at some pretty dark and sometimes misogynistic statements of his. "Notes of a Dirty Old Man" is definitely not a book for the politically correct. <br/><br/>The first half was off to a slow start, a lot of rambles and rants, one after the other. I</span>
  <span id="freeText583493738964709683" style="display:none">This is not my favourite of Bukowski's, yet "classic Bukowski" it was indeed (I missed the old bastard). <br /><br />I am not one to be easily offended (especially when it comes to the brutally honest, non-romantisized works of Bukowski) yet I found myself gasping in horror at some pretty dark and sometimes misogynistic statements of his. "Notes of a Dirty Old Man" is definitely not a book for the politically correct. <br /><br />The first half was off to a slow start, a lot of rambles and rants, one after the other. It was not uninteresting yet not as gripping as I would have liked it to be. In the second half Bukowski gives me a reason to listen, to closely pay attention, as he elaborates on numerous stories and experiences, weaving together one book of separate stories, yes, but not without context. <br /><br />The first half was the hardest to get into, that's why it took me over 2 months to finish Reading, but for the last 100 pages or so, I couldn't get his stories and words out of my head.</span>
  <a data-text-id="583493738964709683" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_797187297" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/797187297" />
    <a title="Cody Sexton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3452713-cody-sexton"><img alt="Cody Sexton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556077120p2/3452713.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/797187297?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cody Sexton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cody Sexton" href="/user/show/3452713-cody-sexton">Cody Sexton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer797187297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7366489870293290436">Bukowski is the writer other writers try to emulate.<br/>Bukowski is often accused of stripping the good away from life but if you read closer you'll realize how much respect he has for life and how much joy it brings him. I wouldn't describe his work as realism only as a type of "subjective realism" where the truth of the matter is only true for him. As he himself says, "The public takes from a writer what it needs and let's the remainder go, but what they take is usually what they need least and wh</span>
  <span id="freeText7366489870293290436" style="display:none">Bukowski is the writer other writers try to emulate.<br />Bukowski is often accused of stripping the good away from life but if you read closer you'll realize how much respect he has for life and how much joy it brings him. I wouldn't describe his work as realism only as a type of "subjective realism" where the truth of the matter is only true for him. As he himself says, "The public takes from a writer what it needs and let's the remainder go, but what they take is usually what they need least and what they let go is what they need the most."</span>
  <a data-text-id="7366489870293290436" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2509870695" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2509870695" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40159504-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604260353p2/40159504.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/40159504-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753513828" href="/book/show/3038407-notes-of-a-dirty-old-man">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2509870695" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14075561716302825670">Raw, Filthy, and confusing. This book takes a little bit to pick up but it's worth the read if you like stream of consciousness type deals. I enjoyed this but not as much as the poetry. I took off a few stars for what felt like lack of organization.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9S73QPQMkng8a9gu4+Y1Qa0OggdV22ZOUrmGgTP+dyCmUFVCqIfU/ocHey2LziBIfX7p6tR9FKnUoZ3B/Bovgg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('50cRy8PzSFZTGG+CqHb/DZ5fMQrrcOoX+GQSM9SuAt20ObPJn3gO0Oh0zIHAXuoETi9a52rWmPB+fAlzG0pafw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>
